<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11574195620365836060">One by one, five sixteen-year-old orphans are brought to a strange building. It is not a prison, not a hospital; it has no walls, no ceiling, no floor. Nothing but endless flights of stairs leading nowhere ?except back to a strange red machine. The five must learn to love the machine and let it rule their lives. But will they let it kill their souls?</span>

      </div>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer160762345">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8683789822317792348">As a kid I was <em>addicted</em> to this book, which is fairly impressive given that at eight years old I doubt I understood half the words Sleator was using. ("Eventually we will have techniques to deal with individuals even as intractable as you"? Not so much.) <br /><br />But seriously. I loved the cover, with the kids flinging themselves in all directions. I loved the semi-dystopian description of the future--never quite explained, but just *there* enough to be creepy. I loved the terrifyingly endless maze of st</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8683789822317792348">As a kid I was <em>addicted</em> to this book, which is fairly impressive given that at eight years old I doubt I understood half the words Sleator was using. ("Eventually we will have techniques to deal with individuals even as intractable as you"? Not so much.) <br /><br />But seriously. I loved the cover, with the kids flinging themselves in all directions. I loved the semi-dystopian description of the future--never quite explained, but just *there* enough to be creepy. I loved the terrifyingly endless maze of stairs, and I loved the way the kids turned on each other like rabid animals. And just in case you didn't get the part about my feelings towards this book, I loved loved <em>loved</em> the ending. It's at that point that you realize the adults, who don't show up until the very end, were <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[with permission from the President trying to create an army of brainwashed super-soldiers who could be conditioned to follow <em>any order</em> regardless of their personal beliefs. When Blossom, Abigail and Oliver hit the blinking green light and immediately start dancing, because that was the particular Pavlovian response they'd been programmed with, and you suddenly realize that they're going to be that way FOREVER... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> guys, that's awesome.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

      <a href="/user/show/1594071-lstirl" class="user" title="Lstirl">Lstirl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38998744">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15128447273403665133">A chilling and suspenseful tale that stick with the reader for years to come.<br /><br />This book is recommended for 9-12 year olds, however, I think it might be a bit intense for the younger side of this group, and I, as an adult, thoroughly enjoyed Slater's treatment of this psychological horror.<br /><br />Personal Note: I read this book as a tween, and it stuck with me all these years. I remember not being able to put it down, and upon revisiting it, it is still just as fascinating to me. I read many of the Amazon</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15128447273403665133">A chilling and suspenseful tale that stick with the reader for years to come.<br /><br />This book is recommended for 9-12 year olds, however, I think it might be a bit intense for the younger side of this group, and I, as an adult, thoroughly enjoyed Slater's treatment of this psychological horror.<br /><br />Personal Note: I read this book as a tween, and it stuck with me all these years. I remember not being able to put it down, and upon revisiting it, it is still just as fascinating to me. I read many of the Amazon.com reviews, and so many of them were from adults that still remember the impact this book had on them.<br /><br />My Review:<br />This is an incredibly creative and original science fiction tale that chronicles the events that take place when five teen-aged orphans are behaviorally trained to respond to a machine. Similarly to "Lord of the Flies," anarchy rules, and the reader is able to view the dark side of humanity when left to its own devices. This might be a new exploration for the tween, who, for the most part, has been exposed to more heartwarming tales. There is nothing warm and fuzzy in this book. It is sheer dark and cold, though we do see triumph of inner character from some of the players. The author draws the reader in immediately, and through crafty writing, is able to keep the reader right at the edge, unable to put the book down. <br /><br />This book is a great introduction to science fiction and psychological thrillers. A unique and memorable tale, the terrifying tale is one that could be enjoyed by older tweens. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15128447273403665133">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_74449080">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74449080" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1712843-keeley" class="left imgcol" title="Keeley"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1228737722p2/1712843.jpg" alt="Keeley" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/74449080?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 13, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1712843-keeley" class="user" title="Keeley">Keeley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1712843-keeley?shelf=read-but-don-t-own" class="actionLinkLite">read-but-don-t-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer74449080">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15556835365042351367">After googling different phrases for a while, I finally found the name of the book I read when I was around 12 that catapulted me into my interest into psychology and the human mind. The story of five teenagers locked in a place that consisted only of stairs and landings, the way they were trained like Pavlov's dogs to respond to the demands of a machine for food, demands that became ever more horrible. I remember reading this and being horrified by concepts I couldn't quite yet really grasp, bu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15556835365042351367">After googling different phrases for a while, I finally found the name of the book I read when I was around 12 that catapulted me into my interest into psychology and the human mind. The story of five teenagers locked in a place that consisted only of stairs and landings, the way they were trained like Pavlov's dogs to respond to the demands of a machine for food, demands that became ever more horrible. I remember reading this and being horrified by concepts I couldn't quite yet really grasp, but what stuck with me was the fact that not everyone acquiesced. The last scene of the book, with the traffic light, I think cemented my firm regard and defense of individuality, even before I could verbalize that notion. While I didn't understand it, really, as a kid, this book and the message in its story has remained with me my entire life. This would definitely be listed as one of the Impact Books in my life. I haven't re-read it as an adult yet, but I will be. It will be interesting to see how it speaks now.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15556835365042351367">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_53912049">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53912049" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/858949-chris" class="left imgcol" title="Chris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208350750p2/858949.jpg" alt="Chris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/53912049?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 25, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/858949-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=top-shelf" class="actionLinkLite">top-shelf</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=psychology" class="actionLinkLite">psychology</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer53912049">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13407168376320666445">Young adult fiction must be a really tough genre to wrap your head around, for a writer. You have a story that you want to tell, and you have to tell it in such a way that it is simple enough for your target audience to read, yet engaging enough to keep them reading. The themes have to be familiar enough for them to understand and relate to, yet unusual enough to be interesting for them. Go too far in the wrong direction and you have a failure. So how does a YA writer do it, balancing all those</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13407168376320666445">Young adult fiction must be a really tough genre to wrap your head around, for a writer. You have a story that you want to tell, and you have to tell it in such a way that it is simple enough for your target audience to read, yet engaging enough to keep them reading. The themes have to be familiar enough for them to understand and relate to, yet unusual enough to be interesting for them. Go too far in the wrong direction and you have a failure. So how does a YA writer do it, balancing all those issues, while still writing a good book?<br /><br />Damned if I know. I've never managed to write a decent book for <em>adults</em>, much less young ones.<br /><br />Fortunately, there are plenty of talented writers who can write for young people, and one of those is William Sleator.<br /><br />A YA writer who specializes in science fiction, Sleator has written his fair share of strange, fantastic and sometimes disturbing books. Of all the ones I've read, this book is probably the one that creeped me out the most.<br /><br />The setup for this story is simple. Five sixteen year-old orphans - two boys, three girls - are put into a giant room, with no visible walls, ceiling or floor. The only structures in this room are stairs and landings. Nothing else except for a small machine with flashing lights and odd sounds that dispenses food.<br /><br />That's it.<br /><br />The five characters are very different and very interesting. First we have Peter, a scared boy, uncertain of his surroundings in the best of times, and utterly overwhelmed by being dropped into this bizarre place. He's afraid of everything and everybody, and finds solace only his the strange trances he drops into, in which he is with an old orphanage roommate, Jasper, feeling safe and protected. As an interesting aside, it wasn't until I was much older that I figured out Peter's sexuality. It wasn't that thinly veiled, either. I really don't handle subtlety well, I think....<br /><br />Lola is not a showgirl. Sorry, had to put that in. Lola is a tough, street-smart girl who has no tolerance for stupidity or cruelty. She's had to learn a lot in her time, and doesn't look to others to decide what she should or should not do.<br /><br />Blossom is a fat little girl who is the first to figure out how to use the food dispenser (in a rage at it, she sticks out her tongue, and out pops a food pellet - but more on this later). She is cunning and devious, much sharper than people would give her credit for being. If anyone is truly dangerous in this crowd, it is her.<br /><br />Abagail is a mousy girl, pretty in her own way, but with very little in the way of self-confidence. She tends to latch on to other people and question her own thoughts and actions. She does have compassion, however, though not the means to make her compassion a reality.<br /><br />Finally, Oliver is the other boy of the group, and he is all that Peter is not. He is strong and confident and good-looking. For a while, Peter thinks that Oliver is his old friend, Jasper, and subsequently Peter is devoted to Oliver. A certain power stucture evolves when it is discovered that of all the people, only Oliver can bring Peter out of his trances. Oliver has power, and he is not afraid to use it.<br /><br />These five kids are trapped in this house of stairs. None of them know why they're there, they only know that they are. They soon discover that the food-dispensing machine will only give them food under certain conditions. In the beginning , they are forced to repeat a series of actions and movements, that evolve into a kind of dance, hoping to get food from the machine.<br /><br />From there it gets only worse. They soon discover that the dance isn't enough. The infighting that comes naturally becomes essential to their survival, for only when they are cruel or greedy will the machine start flashing its lights and entice them to dance. The question then becomes whether or not the kids will do as the machine wishes, and how long they can hold out against it. Or <em>if</em> they will.<br /><br />This book is disturbing to say the least. It levels some pretty harsh accusations about human nature, not just regarding the kids in the house of stairs, but also regarding the people who put them there. The kids are there for a reason, and not a good one. The whole setup (which is thoroughly, if somewhat clunkily, explained at the end) is about conditioning, and changing people's personality through stimuli and reinforcement to make them behave as desired. Because it demonstrates people, young people in particular, behaving in a manner that displays the truth of their nature, this book has often been compared to <em>Lord of the Flies</em>, and rightly so. <br /><br />In its way, it's even more disturbing than <em>Lord of the Flies</em> - at least the kids in that book had been left to their own devices, as terrible as they were. In this book, the horrors that these five teens go through are part of a deliberate state-sanctioned experiment in human conditioning - a kind of horrible, Pavlovian <em>Breakfast Club.</em> Such is the nature of that experiment that the two children who resisted the conditioning were actually regarded as failures. Upon reflection, the people pulling the strings are far more frightening and disturbing than these poor, manipulated children.<br /><br />If nothing else, the lesson to be learned from this story is simple - be a human being. There are some things that are too important to sacrifice for something as simple and petty as food and acceptance. We must never allow ourselves to be beasts. We have to be human. This has relevance today, when we are debating the ethics of torture - is it a necessary evil that we must tolerate if our society is to survive, or is it an offense against our humanity? If we allow ourselves to be fooled into thinking that an evil act is somehow the right thing to do, then we have lost a very important part of ourselves.<br /><br />Of course, it's also about science, but the message here is less dire - we must not allow science to lose its humanity. In this book, a strange future with a monolithic state government, science is entirely utilitarian, with no moral qualms about putting minors through psychological torture. The good news is that, at least as of this writing, science errs on the side of ethics. Modern science certainly has its moral gray areas, but the majority of scientists out there would never consent to run an experiment such as this. I hope.<br /><br />The last line in the book is one of the more frightening ones in literature, right up there with the last line in 1984. It's a blunt reminder of everything that has happened in the book, and a pointed summation of everything that Sleator has been trying to say - that humans have a base nature, that we can be manipulated, and we will, given the right circumstances, allow others to shape who we are. His message to his readers - teenagers like the ones in this book - is to refuse to submit to such control. Good advice for them, and for us.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13407168376320666445">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_35089436">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35089436" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1539516-diana-welsch" class="left imgcol" title="Diana Welsch"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442520465p2/1539516.jpg" alt="Diana Welsch" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/35089436?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1539516-diana-welsch" class="user" title="Diana Welsch">Diana Welsch</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1539516-diana-welsch?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer35089436">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6913083370017172807">I read this because it is the #1 answer to "What the hell was that crazy book I read 20 years ago?" questions. House of Stairs has showed up on LibraryThing's Name That Book group three times in the past year. Half of the YA books in that group turn out to be William Sleator. We got a Contact Us question the other day that turned out to be 2 William Sleator books. So I figured it was high time I read something by this mysterious man.<br /><br />Jesus. There's a reason people remember it. This book was crazy</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6913083370017172807">I read this because it is the #1 answer to "What the hell was that crazy book I read 20 years ago?" questions. House of Stairs has showed up on LibraryThing's Name That Book group three times in the past year. Half of the YA books in that group turn out to be William Sleator. We got a Contact Us question the other day that turned out to be 2 William Sleator books. So I figured it was high time I read something by this mysterious man.<br /><br />Jesus. There's a reason people remember it. This book was crazy. I read it in about 2 hours and could not put it down.<br /><br />In a sucky future America where everyone lives in huge run-down apartment buildings and subsists on synthetic food, 5 teenage orphans of various personality types find themselves the subject of a sinister psychological experiment. They are blindfolded, taken from their group homes and dumped in a huge, brightly-lit building filled with neverending stairs, bridges and landings. There is one toilet (which is also the only source of water) in the middle of a narrow, vertigo-inducing bridge. Most importantly, there is a machine with flashing lights and sounds that sometimes dispenses food contingent on certain behaviors from the teens.<br /><br />Lola (the badass), Peter (the shrinking violet), Blossom (the spoiled, self-centered brat), Abigail (the pleaser), and Oliver (the alpha-male) have to figure out how to behave to get the machine to feed them. For a while, it feeds them if they all cooperate on an elaborate dance when the lights start flashing. But what happens when the machine begins to demand other kinds of behavior from them?<br /><br />I was worried that this book would have an anticlimatic ending, that there would be no explanation of why this was happening to these people. But it has a very satisfying ending that will leave you thinking about it for a long time. It's like <a title="1984" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=1984">1984</a> for a slightly younger set.<br /><br />A fantastic book, but so disturbing I wouldn't recommend it to anyone younger than about 14.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6913083370017172807">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus" class="left imgcol" title="Deborah Markus"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442990055p2/954028.jpg" alt="Deborah Markus" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/697368641?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus" class="user" title="Deborah Markus">Deborah Markus</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6152195-house-of-stairs" title="Library Binding 9780833559852" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=modern-classics" class="actionLinkLite">modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer697368641">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1099613550307637563">A modern classic. I read <em>House of Stairs</em> frequently as a child, and was happy to see this speculative novel from 1974 stands the test of time.<br /><br />That's because Sleator wisely keeps the "futuristic" science fiction touches to a minimum. There's a very '70s reference to air pollution, but it isn't pivotal. The rest of the book is about what doesn't change: human nature.<br /><br />I'm handing this to my son to read now. I have the feeling he'll be as riveted by it as I was at his age.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_171327232">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171327232" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1416912-trudi" class="left imgcol" title="Trudi"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" alt="Trudi" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/171327232?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 05, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1416912-trudi" class="user" title="Trudi">Trudi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">dystopia</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=survive-this" class="actionLinkLite">survive-this</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=crime-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=pulpy-goodness" class="actionLinkLite">pulpy-goodness</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=love-the-premise" class="actionLinkLite">love-the-premise</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=group-in-peril" class="actionLinkLite">group-in-peril</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer171327232">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1351383939094684591">I am insanely <s>addicted</s> attracted to stories about "the group in peril", when people are thrust into an alien setting absent of any social rules and obligations. Under such circumstances, it usually doesn't take long for humans to throw off the shackles of civilized conduct and resort to a more brutal "survival of the fittest" approach. That’s not just the pessimist in me coming out, but the realist. <br /><br />What we become <em>in extremis</em> is both fascinating and frightening in the heroic heights we reach and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1351383939094684591">I am insanely <s>addicted</s> attracted to stories about "the group in peril", when people are thrust into an alien setting absent of any social rules and obligations. Under such circumstances, it usually doesn't take long for humans to throw off the shackles of civilized conduct and resort to a more brutal "survival of the fittest" approach. That’s not just the pessimist in me coming out, but the realist. <br /><br />What we become <em>in extremis</em> is both fascinating and frightening in the heroic heights we reach and the craven depths we sink to, and how quickly we revert to our most primal and baser urges. One hundred thousand years of evolution gone in the blink of an eye. William Golding shows us this in <a title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies">Lord of the Flies</a>, as does Scott Smith in <a title="The Ruins by Scott B. Smith" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21726.The_Ruins">The Ruins</a>, Jose Saramago in <a title="Blindness by José Saramago" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness">Blindness</a> and Stephen King in his novella <a title="The Mist by Stephen King" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/813214.The_Mist">The Mist</a>. These books teach us that there are even worse fates than losing your life – it's losing your humanity.<br /><br />In <em>House of Stairs</em>, William Sleator proves just how quickly humans can be stripped of their humanity. First published in 1974, I imagine Sleator was influenced at least in part, by some of the more famous psych experiments of the first half of the 20th century including the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Little_Albert_experiment">Little Albert Experiment</a> and the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milgram_experiment">Milgram Obedience Experiment</a>. Just a few years prior to its publication there was also the infamous Stanford Prison Experiment – a study designed to ostensibly observe the effects of becoming either a prisoner or prison guard. Twenty-four students were selected out of 75 to play the prisoners and live in a mock prison in the basement of the Stanford psychology building. Roles were assigned randomly. This “experiment” degenerated so rapidly into violence and the dehumanization of its subjects that it had to be stopped after only six days. Good times. <br /><br />The five 16-year-old protagonists here are subjected to much the same mindfuck (pardon my French), enclosed in a never-ending space of stairs – there are no walls, no floors, no doors, no ceiling, just stairs, going up, going down. That’s the set-up. What follows is pretty tame by today’s standards, and in my books does not hold a candle to <a title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies">Lord of the Flies</a>; however, it still makes for pure, unadulterated compulsive reading. It doesn’t surprise me that in 2000, the American Library Association, with teen participation, chose it as one of the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://home.comcast.net/%7Eantaylor1/alabestteens.html">100 Best Young Adult Books of the last 50 years</a>. Recommended!!<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1351383939094684591">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer166935004">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16260390088738657756">I think this is the last entry in my YA Sci-Fi kick, but it's a high note to end on. I've spent more than half my life searching for this book. Really! I checked it out from the Lawrence Public Library when I was 9 or 10 and read the first 15 pages, and then it got away from me. I remember everything about it vividly--5 teenage orphans in a near-future distopia find themselves, without explanation, in a gigantic white room consisting only of endless staircases and a machine that irregularly disp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16260390088738657756">I think this is the last entry in my YA Sci-Fi kick, but it's a high note to end on. I've spent more than half my life searching for this book. Really! I checked it out from the Lawrence Public Library when I was 9 or 10 and read the first 15 pages, and then it got away from me. I remember everything about it vividly--5 teenage orphans in a near-future distopia find themselves, without explanation, in a gigantic white room consisting only of endless staircases and a machine that irregularly dispenses sausage when one character sticks out her tongue--except for the title and author! And how could I go to a librarian and describe the convoluted plot of an obscure 70's kids book? For some reason, I thought I couldn't. So I would think about this book from time to time for the next 18 years, despairingly, and then I was reading a review of The Hunger Games in the New Yorker, and Laura Miller gave a quick summary of the genre of YA distopic sci-fi, and described this book with complete citations! So I retrieved it from the library, probably the exact same copy I read when I was 9, and I read it in a few hours, and it's great! Simple and scary, with both inspiring and disturbing implications about the human soul. Just what kids want. Now that I've settled this part of my childhood, I can continue reading the metafiction of my agegroup. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5432056651275179623"> My, my, my, what a chilling book. <br /><br />Five strangers - all orphans, all sixteen years of age - are released, blindfolded, into a mammoth gleaming white room full of stairs. They find each other and congregate around a machine that dispenses food upon correct behavioural patterns. Soon they are slaves to this machine, searching for the correct patterns, doing whatever they have to do to be fed. What follows is your classic deterioration of human nature.<br /><br />Reading it, I was reminded a lot of the Maze Ru</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5432056651275179623"> My, my, my, what a chilling book. <br /><br />Five strangers - all orphans, all sixteen years of age - are released, blindfolded, into a mammoth gleaming white room full of stairs. They find each other and congregate around a machine that dispenses food upon correct behavioural patterns. Soon they are slaves to this machine, searching for the correct patterns, doing whatever they have to do to be fed. What follows is your classic deterioration of human nature.<br /><br />Reading it, I was reminded a lot of the Maze Runner - kids being manipulated by some unseen beings, forced into horrible situations, doing what needs to be done to survive, etc. Obviously, House of Stairs came first, and I actually feel that it is better than the aforementioned MZ (more so when summing up the series as a whole) because here we have this fantastic novel that is not even two hundred pages long, and yet it is a completely realised, satisfying read. The ending is very good also.<br /><br />I was going to give this four stars, but after finishing it I realised that it is the kind of book I will probably read again (and again?) so I'll go all the way and give it five.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13385278878279890609">A bit dated more than 40 years and with a dedication which hints at the climax, but still....a very effective creepy tale and a parable which ought to leave the reader wondering: just why do I do things the way I do?<br /><br />William Sleator was perhaps the perfect young adult author. He originally set out to write for adults, but shied from using excessive profanity and other elements "required" by publishers in adult novels. So....he wrote adult stories (for the most part) and populated them with teenag</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13385278878279890609">A bit dated more than 40 years and with a dedication which hints at the climax, but still....a very effective creepy tale and a parable which ought to leave the reader wondering: just why do I do things the way I do?<br /><br />William Sleator was perhaps the perfect young adult author. He originally set out to write for adults, but shied from using excessive profanity and other elements "required" by publishers in adult novels. So....he wrote adult stories (for the most part) and populated them with teenagers. "House Of Stairs" was his first book and may be his masterpiece.<br /><br />Using the familiar "5 characters in search of an exit" theme, "House Of Stairs" quickly moves on to several levels of actions, both inward and outward. I don't want to give away the story or the ending--let's just say that it turns out that the five characters are in a place no human being should be, for a purpose no human being should be subjected to.<br /><br />Read and see for yourself. You just might guess what is going on partway through. But even so, I will bet that the last line will make you shiver--and that you'll never forget it. If you've read the book thoroughly. Don't peek!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13385278878279890609">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_474416254">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/474416254" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/781364-roybot" class="left imgcol" title="Roybot"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1339008721p2/781364.jpg" alt="Roybot" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/474416254?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 10, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/781364-roybot" class="user" title="Roybot">Roybot</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/3629010-house-of-stairs" title="Hardcover 9780525323358" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer474416254">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11642706183379635195">Several years ago, I saw a movie called <em>The Cube.</em> It's a sort of low-budget thriller/horror movie about a group of strangers who find themselves trapped in a maze of cube-like rooms full of traps. As I watched it, I found myself having a strong sense of deja-vu. Where had I seen this before? Why was the premise so familiar? <br /><br />By the end of the movie, I was convinced that I had not seen it before, but had, instead, read something that was extremely similar. I was sure that <em>The Cube</em> was basically a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11642706183379635195">Several years ago, I saw a movie called <em>The Cube.</em> It's a sort of low-budget thriller/horror movie about a group of strangers who find themselves trapped in a maze of cube-like rooms full of traps. As I watched it, I found myself having a strong sense of deja-vu. Where had I seen this before? Why was the premise so familiar? <br /><br />By the end of the movie, I was convinced that I had not seen it before, but had, instead, read something that was extremely similar. I was sure that <em>The Cube</em> was basically a film adaptation of a short story or novel, but, no matter who I talked to, nobody seemed to remember a short story like that. For years, I kept trying to figure out what I had read that was so similar to <em>The Cube</em> (spoiler: It was <em>House of Stairs</em>)<br /><br />Finally, just last week, I was searching for it again, and there it was. Five teens, trapped, not in cubes, but in an infinte stairway. <br /><br />I immediately sent away for a copy from my local library (Hi Minuteman!). <br /><br />Having reread it, it's safe to say that my memory of the book wasn't quite accurate, but it's a story that seems to have held up over time, quite well. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The experiment is just as disturbing now as I remember it being. There are certain points--where Lola is working with Peter and his trances--that were a little obvious, but, then, I'm reading the book as a 33 year-old adult who is more than passingly familiar with the idea of Pavlovian responses. I suspect that 16 year-old me probably appreciated seeing the other side of the training. <br /><br />It was a useful counter to the speed with which Abigail, Oliver, and Blossom turn on each other. It's already a pretty bleak book, in many ways, but it would have been almost unbearably so to have everyone willing to shove their humanity aside and embrace the cruelty that they were required to exhibit in order to get food pellets. While it's a little far fetched that Peter comes out of the stairs not only able to resist the temptation to give in to despair, but as a healthier, more well adjusted person, I think that it makes the book better, and provides some germ of hope that not everyone is doomed to be an Oliver, Abigail, or Blossom. <br /><br />I was also pleased to see that, despite the fact that 99% of the book takes place in a nightmare world of endless stairs, Sleater sneaks a lot of world building in--we know that the book is set in dystopian future where most of the population live in a giant apartment megacomplexes, and where the planet's evironment has been all but destroyed (people still drive, but must wear gasmasks outside, and real meat is a luxury that only the megawealthy can afford).  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Overall, a very cool sci-fi read. Very glad that I was able to figure out what it was called!<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11642706183379635195">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_105935481">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105935481" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/270740-meagan" class="left imgcol" title="Meagan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1186969219p2/270740.jpg" alt="Meagan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/105935481?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 06, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/270740-meagan" class="user" title="Meagan">Meagan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/270740-meagan?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/270740-meagan?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/270740-meagan?shelf=suspense" class="actionLinkLite">suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer105935481">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9382786508025584306">William Sleator's suspenseful, almost sci-fi, novel is pretty old (even older than me, but don't tell anyone), but I think its lack of specific details means that it holds up pretty well. I also think that it's a good companion read for one of my favorites, <em>The Hunger Games</em>. Both books have a similar tone, although <em>The Hunger Games</em> is more tightly written, fast-paced, and suspenseful. What Sleator's book has is a really thought-provoking setup that asks you to think about humanity and how easily</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9382786508025584306">William Sleator's suspenseful, almost sci-fi, novel is pretty old (even older than me, but don't tell anyone), but I think its lack of specific details means that it holds up pretty well. I also think that it's a good companion read for one of my favorites, <em>The Hunger Games</em>. Both books have a similar tone, although <em>The Hunger Games</em> is more tightly written, fast-paced, and suspenseful. What Sleator's book has is a really thought-provoking setup that asks you to think about humanity and how easily we can turn on each other.<br /><br />The setup is this: a teenage orphan boy is blindfolded and taken to a secret location. When the blindfold is removed he finds himself in a massive room with seemingly no ceiling, walls, or floor. The room is painted blindingly white and is filled with stairs. There are no railings, and only the tiniest landings with the occasional thin bridge spanning open spaces. Before too long he comes across a girl who has also been left in this strange environment, then another two girls and a boy. These five teens, all orphans, have been abandoned in this strange environment with only a strange machine that feeds them only when they do certain things. The things it's asking them to do, though, get stranger and stranger.<br /><br />It's a short book, so I think it's worthwhile for fans of <em>The Hunger Games</em> to give this old school book a try.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9382786508025584306">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer119502177">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12933711150379928385">A gripping psycholgical tale. Five orphans caugt up and manipulated in a terrifying experiment. <br /><br />I cannot say I liked it--in the sense it is not meant to be liked. It is meant to make one think and that it does.<br /><br />To me one disturbing thing is--in no way did I feel "This could never happen". Knowing what inhumane things HAVe been done, nothing like this 'experiment' actually happening would surprise me.<br /><br />In only 176 pages, William Sleator delivers an unforgettable tale.<br /><br />Btw, although often recommended</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12933711150379928385">A gripping psycholgical tale. Five orphans caugt up and manipulated in a terrifying experiment. <br /><br />I cannot say I liked it--in the sense it is not meant to be liked. It is meant to make one think and that it does.<br /><br />To me one disturbing thing is--in no way did I feel "This could never happen". Knowing what inhumane things HAVe been done, nothing like this 'experiment' actually happening would surprise me.<br /><br />In only 176 pages, William Sleator delivers an unforgettable tale.<br /><br />Btw, although often recommended for teens, I would recommend age 15 or older, due to the intensity of the writing.<br /><br />Highly recommend for anyone desiring an intriguing story; especially recommended if you like psychological suspense.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1309138728">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1309138728" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6217162-donna" class="left imgcol" title="Donna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1430955688p2/6217162.jpg" alt="Donna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1309138728?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 17, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6217162-donna" class="user" title="Donna">Donna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6217162-donna?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6217162-donna?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1309138728">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer169477656016111144">This YA book, written in a simplistic style and old fashioned in tone, like an old Hardy Boys or Nancy Drew mystery, had a complex story beneath the story, one of a sinister kind. This book reminded me of a Twilight Zone episode where a group of unsuspecting people are at the mercy of something or someone working against them. It's Lord of the Flies meets 1984 with a twist. <br /><br />On the surface, not much happens as five 16 year old orphans are removed from their living situations, blindfolded, and aba</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText169477656016111144">This YA book, written in a simplistic style and old fashioned in tone, like an old Hardy Boys or Nancy Drew mystery, had a complex story beneath the story, one of a sinister kind. This book reminded me of a Twilight Zone episode where a group of unsuspecting people are at the mercy of something or someone working against them. It's Lord of the Flies meets 1984 with a twist. <br /><br />On the surface, not much happens as five 16 year old orphans are removed from their living situations, blindfolded, and abandoned in a place made only of free floating stairs linked by bridges. But while there is little physical action, much is happening within each teen to move the story forward. The teens each represent a stereotypical personality, from intellectual to bossy to wimpy to greedy to pleaser. Why are they in this situation and why them in particular? Is this a punishment or an experiment or a torture device? All the teens know is that whatever it is, they must survive it. <br /><br />I won't say more to avoid spoilers. But I will say that this was a nasty little horror story with a lesson or two about human nature. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="169477656016111144">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1181908262">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1181908262" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20388365-wendy-bousfield" class="left imgcol" title="Wendy Bousfield"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394119553p2/20388365.jpg" alt="Wendy Bousfield" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1181908262?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20388365-wendy-bousfield" class="user" title="Wendy Bousfield">Wendy Bousfield</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Wendy by: </span>
      Michele
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20388365-wendy-bousfield?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20388365-wendy-bousfield?shelf=dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1181908262">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11796562956088489590">This young adult novel (1974) reflects Cold War paranoia: apprehension that the government will brainwash unwilling subjects into perfectly controllable weapons of war. Five sixteen-year-old orphans find themselves in a sterile maze-like environment, consisting entirely of stairs and landings. (Wikipedia notes that the setting is based on Escher’s (1953) lithograph: Relativity: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.scottmcd.net/artanalysis/?p=548" target="_blank">http://www.scottmcd.net/artanalysis/?...</a>). Besides one pool of water (toilet, drinking water, and bath), a machine with</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11796562956088489590">This young adult novel (1974) reflects Cold War paranoia: apprehension that the government will brainwash unwilling subjects into perfectly controllable weapons of war. Five sixteen-year-old orphans find themselves in a sterile maze-like environment, consisting entirely of stairs and landings. (Wikipedia notes that the setting is based on Escher’s (1953) lithograph: Relativity: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.scottmcd.net/artanalysis/?p=548" target="_blank">http://www.scottmcd.net/artanalysis/?...</a>). Besides one pool of water (toilet, drinking water, and bath), a machine with flashing lights sporadically dispenses pellets of food in response to behaviors that, through trial and error, the children learn. At first, the machine ejects food when the children engage in a stylized dance. As time goes by, however, the machine will only produce food if, in addition to the dance, the children act aggressively toward one another. When it becomes clear that they will be fed only by escalating cruelty, the children divide into two groups. Remaining near the machine, Oliver, Abigail, and Blossom create for themselves a kind of Lord of the Flies hierarchy. These three continue to eat by finding ways to inflict pain on one another. Peter and Lola, in contrast, remove themselves to a distant part of the maze, supporting one another in the decision to starve rather than become monsters.<br /><br />Lola and Peter are emotionally engaging characters. A tough nonconformist, Lola smokes and gleefully reminisces about tricks she played on the repressive keepers at her orphanage. She responds to an alien environment by exploring, seeking to learn the rules that govern it, and jogging to keep fit. Peter, a timid, insecure child, retreats into a dream “room”: a memory of an orphanage where his friend Jasper loved and cared for him. When Peter meets Oliver, he develops a crush on this handsome, narcissistic boy. Delicately, the book hints that Peter’s attraction to both boys is sexual. Peter and Lola, however, strengthen one another in resisting corruption.<br /><br />A major weakness in HOUSE OF STAIRS, however, is its depiction of the villains, Oliver and Blossom. They do not change in any way because both are calculating, cruel, and self-absorbed from the beginning. Though the machine rewards their cruel actions, it does not corrupt them: Oliver and Blosson are already monstrous. The privileged daughter of government officials, obese Blossom seeks only to make the machine produce as much food as possible and spitefully to turn the other children against one another. At the beginning, Oliver sets out to shame and manipulate insecure, passive Abigail, alternately treating her seductively and indifferently. Because most of us justify evil actions with self-deceptions and rationalizations, I found Oliver and Blossom’s single-mindedness wooden and unbelievable. <br /><br />Until the very end, the HOUSE OF STAIRS only hints obliquely at the dystopian world outside the environment in which the children are trapped. Only the most elite, like Blossom’s family, live in single-family houses. The other 99% struggle to subsist in an overpopulated world, where nourishing food is scarce. Because even children of rich parents end up in orphanages, the novel suggests human life has become so cheap that children are not cherished. HOUSE OF STAIRS reflects Cold War fears that the government was conducting dehumanizing experiments on human beings to advance the war effort. <br /><br />Reading HOUSE OF STAIRS in 2015, I found it a period piece, reflecting old-fashioned preoccupations and literary tropes. (Of course, we DO face the problem of returning servicemen who, successfully conditioned to regard Arab opponents as non-human, do not undergo “unconditioning” before returning to civilian life.) I was glad to be introduced to William Sleator (1945-2011), but do not think I shall seek out other novels.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11796562956088489590">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_10204443">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10204443" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/665966-rico" class="left imgcol" title="Rico"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Rico" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/10204443?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 09, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/665966-rico" class="user" title="Rico">Rico</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10204443">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17849738129640621931">Taut juvenile fiction novel. It's a Lord-of-the-Flies-esque tale of a group of orphans who find themselves mysteriously left alone in a bizarre prison of endlessly interlocking stairs. Slowly they're turned against each other by a Pavlovian "food machine" that rewards violence. I won't give away the ending, but alas, adult readers will probably quickly figure out what's going on. As a teen, though, it made a real impression, and its message is maybe more relevant now than ever.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_56573775">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56573775" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/504256-lizzi-crystal" class="left imgcol" title="Lizzi Crystal"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441524959p2/504256.jpg" alt="Lizzi Crystal" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/56573775?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/504256-lizzi-crystal" class="user" title="Lizzi Crystal">Lizzi Crystal</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer56573775">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8075937665625058847">Not 5 stars for my enjoyment of the story, which gave me nightmares, but it was a <em>perfect</em> telling of the story. I would not have changed one thing. Stories of this genre are often told in crass, even vile ways, and I so much appreciated how the author treated this story about base humanity with dignity and respect, allowing me to focus on the characters and psychology rather than gruesome details. A definite 5 stars for the storytelling! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12642133679335447353">As a Psychology mayor, I have studied and am versed in the risks, benefits and limitations of conditioned reinforcement. To say that the experiment plotted out by the scientist of the book is unequivocally unethical, is to merely state the obvious. But what other experiments have been performed on real-life human beings, with similar, devastating effects? What charades are being performed even now, controling the population through study and marketing, through communication and the control of in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12642133679335447353">As a Psychology mayor, I have studied and am versed in the risks, benefits and limitations of conditioned reinforcement. To say that the experiment plotted out by the scientist of the book is unequivocally unethical, is to merely state the obvious. But what other experiments have been performed on real-life human beings, with similar, devastating effects? What charades are being performed even now, controling the population through study and marketing, through communication and the control of information, through the very nature of the human being and it's innate willingness to be controlled? In control lays security, be it by virtue of being in control or by being the controlled. And denying that is dangerously naive. But who controls who? And by whom?<br />I read this one in a little under three hours. But the impression it left in me will surely last a lot longer than that.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1193149706">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1193149706" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/34722038-kristen" class="left imgcol" title="Kristen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1417043914p2/34722038.jpg" alt="Kristen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1193149706?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/34722038-kristen" class="user" title="Kristen">Kristen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer1193149706'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1193149706">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16438755038517429982">I read this book the first time in grade school and something about it always stuck with me. I decided to revisit it as an adult and was not disappointed. <br /><br />Five 16 year old orphans find themselves in a bizarre room. No walls, ceilings or floors; just an endless maze of stairs. They have no idea why they're there or what their purpose. As escape or rescue becomes clearly impossible, food now becomes a primary concern. Fortunately there is a strange machine that is capable of feeding them all, if t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16438755038517429982">I read this book the first time in grade school and something about it always stuck with me. I decided to revisit it as an adult and was not disappointed. <br /><br />Five 16 year old orphans find themselves in a bizarre room. No walls, ceilings or floors; just an endless maze of stairs. They have no idea why they're there or what their purpose. As escape or rescue becomes clearly impossible, food now becomes a primary concern. Fortunately there is a strange machine that is capable of feeding them all, if they can figure out how it works. As endless days of monotony begin to take its toll, it becomes more and more apparent what the machine wants them to do: hurt each other. And as time drags on they find more and more creative and disturbing ways of doing just that. <br /><br />I really liked how the book starts you off just as confused and out of sorts as Peter. The backgrounds of the teenagers all come out through the course of the story as you start to understand why each of them was chosen for this project. Starting with Peter, it seems like he was chosen to see if someone as weak and ineffectual as he would simply follow along with the others. Maybe he was meant to be the punching bag. The same could be said about Abigail as I think Oliver was meant to seduce her. On the surface he's a good looking All-American type but clearly he's just a sociopath. Blossom is just such a detestable, unlikeable character. She was definitely put there to be the catalyst for all the horror that is necessary to make the machine work. And at the center is Lola, the wild card. The unlikely moral compass. <br /><br />There's obviously a lesson to be learned in the putrid behavior of the three and the unification of the two. Peter and Lola become closer and stronger and while Blossom, Oliver and Abigail continue their wretched path. The fact that Peter and Lola are what make the experiment fail is an unsettling view of society. But after just hating Blossom throughout the whole entire book there was some gratification in knowing that her life is just going to get worse from here. I did feel bad for Abigail. It's hard to say which one you would be if you were in the same circumstances. I'd like to think I'd be Lola, but then again I've never woken up in a house of stairs. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16438755038517429982">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1193616374">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1193616374" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/34722018-sean-costello" class="left imgcol" title="Sean Costello"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1435068368p2/34722018.jpg" alt="Sean Costello" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1193616374?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/34722018-sean-costello" class="user" title="Sean Costello">Sean Costello</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer1193616374'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1193616374">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13194920275626851975">I wasn't sure to expect from this story... well except for stairs, but it turned out to be solid little story. It was fairly short which I felt worked in it's favor, keeping the story from getting too monotonous. <em>House of Stairs</em> starts off from the point-of-view of Peter who is dropped off in a strange room of sorts that is filled entirely with stairs and landings. Peter is stumbled upon by a gal named Lola, whom is a tad on the abrasive side, and they set out to explore the expanse of stairs. T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13194920275626851975">I wasn't sure to expect from this story... well except for stairs, but it turned out to be solid little story. It was fairly short which I felt worked in it's favor, keeping the story from getting too monotonous. <em>House of Stairs</em> starts off from the point-of-view of Peter who is dropped off in a strange room of sorts that is filled entirely with stairs and landings. Peter is stumbled upon by a gal named Lola, whom is a tad on the abrasive side, and they set out to explore the expanse of stairs. The two of them then encounter a rather large girl, named Blossom, in the middle of stuffing her face with meat cylinders coming out of a odd machine on the floor, and shortly after two others, Abigail and Oliver, join them. It is established that all these characters are 16 year old orphans and that they were all abducted at their state homes and blind folded till they arrived on the stairs. As they all come together the meat machine no longer responds as it had originally, and the group try and figure it out. Getting it to work is most of what goes on for the rest of the story. They discover that it only works when the lights are flashing and it begins to whisper unintelligibly. Eventually they find it is only possible to get this meat machine to operate via abusing each other which Peter and Lola refuse to take part it. In the end the group is 'rescued' and brought back to a (physically) healthy state and regrouped in a laboratory of some sort where they are told the true meaning behind this experiment. <br /><br /><em>House of Stairs</em> relied heavily on the personalities of the characters, more so than your typical story. I thought it was interesting that, with the exception of Blossom, each person had a doppelganger of sorts. Lola and Oliver were both similar to each other; leader types with very 'loud' personalities. Lola comes across as being particularly determined to find away out and tries to take charge. Though she is the most independent, she needs Peter's help in the end to make it through. Oliver seems to be the prototypical alpha male, until we find out that he is easily overcome by the thoughts of others and eventually succumbs to Blossom's plans due to his bullheaded nature. Then we have Peter and Abigail, who are comparable to one another; thin, soft spoken, in need of someone to lead them along. Peter gets lost in his trances and is very emotional, he becomes transfixed with Oliver because of his similarity to his childhood friend Jasper. He goes on to realize that Oliver is not what nor who he needs and is able to see through the deceptiveness of Blossom, and with Lola's help he really comes into his own as a strong individual. At first it seems Abigail is more resilient character of those two, but as we soon find out she's even more emotionally compromised than Peter was. This leads to Abigail's eventual downfall, even though she appeared the most sensible of the final three. Blossom was the only one who was different from all the other characters in practically every sense. Blossom was affluent up until her only recently becoming an orphan, she was the only fat individual, and the only character I would consider to be genuinely deceptive and deviant in nature. Blossom comes across as a really unlikable character from the start. Her sense of self-righteousness over the other characters, even though they are all on the same level, seems to be what brings about this attitude. In the end it is her wickedness that causes the degeneration of Olivier, Abigail, and herself.<br /><br />I felt a core theme to this story is all about doing what is right. Taking the high ground, regardless of how hard it might be and despite what everyone else may do. From the start I would say Lola is the 'strongest' character and Peter the 'weakest', which is kind of a device to show that anyone is capable doing the right thing. Having the similarities between the characters helps to further this train of thought that 'anyone can do it', since nothing about them <em>really</em> stands out over the others. Because of this Lola and Peter appear to become stronger people at the end of the story. While the others have to suffer the consequences of their actions. Even though they are healthy on the outside they have become mental grotesques, left in a state of being that they no longer have control over.<br /><br />Another, more obvious, theme to this story is that of human conditioning and the false security it provides. We are all conditioned to do certain things over and over to some degree. It is how we function as a society, for the most part, and it keeps most people comfortable and complacent. Abigail, Oliver, and Blossom choosing to stay with what they knew ended up costing them their individuality. <em>House of Stairs</em> helps to show how people never really want to do anything unfamiliar to them, however there are times when you should break free from the typical and do something different for once. How can we improve ourselves if we are cemented in the same cycle? How do you know you do not like something if you have never tried it? Maybe take that left instead of your usual right and see what it can lead to, because before you know it the cycle is complete.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13194920275626851975">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
