<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1367335847">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1367335847" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/901342-jacob" class="left imgcol" title="Jacob"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1228116995p2/901342.jpg" alt="Jacob" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1367335847?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 05, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/901342-jacob" class="user" title="Jacob">Jacob</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6152195-house-of-stairs" title="Library Binding 9780833559852" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=psychology" class="actionLinkLite">psychology</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=science_fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science_fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=youth" class="actionLinkLite">youth</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=from_library" class="actionLinkLite">from_library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1367335847">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8823852467477623196">I didn't remember reading this as a kid until I checked out the book and saw the same cover I'd stared at when I was younger. It's kind of funny because this book is about psychological conditioning and I had a strong recognition reaction to the cover even though, like <a title="Singularity by William Sleator" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24876.Singularity">Singularity</a>, I didn't actually remember any of the plot. In both cases, I think I didn't remember so much as internalize them. In Singularity I identified with the main character's major shift to develop independence, and in this</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8823852467477623196">I didn't remember reading this as a kid until I checked out the book and saw the same cover I'd stared at when I was younger. It's kind of funny because this book is about psychological conditioning and I had a strong recognition reaction to the cover even though, like <a title="Singularity by William Sleator" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24876.Singularity">Singularity</a>, I didn't actually remember any of the plot. In both cases, I think I didn't remember so much as internalize them. In Singularity I identified with the main character's major shift to develop independence, and in this I was fascinated by how Sleator describes one way in which people might be conditioned. I was even more fascinated by the concept that people might refused to be conditioned. Independence again, I guess. Also, it's interesting to me how my life has developed around some of the principles explored in these books, in this case psychology (which I minored in college) and issues of control and choice.<br /><br />The story itself is quite good. I'd rate this 4.5 and might bump it up to 5 if I ever read it again. Sleator puts five teenagers in a giant house of stairs they can't escape and watches them interact. They all have very distinct and believable personalities and motivations, and by the end it's clear they represent some ways in which people deal with adversity in their lives even though Sleator makes them interesting reading for their own sake.<br /><br />It turns out this was first published 10 years earlier than the mid-80's I'd assumed, which makes it more likely the author intended it as a rebuttal to B.F. Skinner, whose influence on psychology was just beginning to wane, and possibly to the fatalist ideas in <a title="1984 by George Orwell" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984">1984</a>. I enjoyed thinking about how these relate to each other, which I didn't get to do when I was younger.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8823852467477623196">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_113345843">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113345843" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1849033-kiri" class="left imgcol" title="Kiri"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1231198786p2/1849033.jpg" alt="Kiri" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/113345843?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 25, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1849033-kiri" class="user" title="Kiri">Kiri</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/468663.House_of_Stairs" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380437863" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1849033-kiri?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1849033-kiri?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer113345843">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1796583396401887183">This is an intriguingly creepy, even chilling, psychological science fiction story. In it, five 16-year-old orphans find themselves in a three-dimensional maze: The House of Stairs. Endless white stairs climb in every direction, with no exit. They gradually learn that the only way to get food is to perform specific actions in front of a machine that responds, sometimes, with a tasty reward. The main dramatic tension arises from the machine's changing responses that shape their behavior in a dire</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1796583396401887183">This is an intriguingly creepy, even chilling, psychological science fiction story. In it, five 16-year-old orphans find themselves in a three-dimensional maze: The House of Stairs. Endless white stairs climb in every direction, with no exit. They gradually learn that the only way to get food is to perform specific actions in front of a machine that responds, sometimes, with a tasty reward. The main dramatic tension arises from the machine's changing responses that shape their behavior in a direction that some of the teens find completely reprehensible --- but others accept. The book is an interesting study of our human reactions to reinforcement, both positive and negative, and the extent of our own inner ethical drives. The writing overall is very engaging, and although the characterization isn't deep, the growing horror behind the situation keeps you turning pages. The final sentence has stayed with me, years after the first time I read it, and it still has a really disturbing finality on re-reading it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1796583396401887183">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_656570862">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656570862" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/975470-sara" class="left imgcol" title="Sara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1389165282p2/975470.jpg" alt="Sara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/656570862?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 07, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/975470-sara" class="user" title="Sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/975470-sara?shelf=young-adult-books" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/975470-sara?shelf=read-in-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/975470-sara?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/975470-sara?shelf=dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">dystopia</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer656570862">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4399868147698368211">I remember loving the mad weirdness of William Sleator's books when I was a teen. I have particularly fond memories for Interstellar Pig (a book I still want to revisit, but am slightly hesitant because I don't know if I'll be able to embrace the bizarre as well as I did when I was 14), so I'm glad that KC forced me to read this with him!<br /><br />KC and I decided to read it out loud together, which was an excellent choice. This book started out feeling a little like The Maze Runner, or even the movie Cub</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4399868147698368211">I remember loving the mad weirdness of William Sleator's books when I was a teen. I have particularly fond memories for Interstellar Pig (a book I still want to revisit, but am slightly hesitant because I don't know if I'll be able to embrace the bizarre as well as I did when I was 14), so I'm glad that KC forced me to read this with him!<br /><br />KC and I decided to read it out loud together, which was an excellent choice. This book started out feeling a little like The Maze Runner, or even the movie Cube, which meant I instantly felt enamored of it. After the initial set up, however, the book totally surprised me by morphing into something different!<br /><br />This is actually a pretty fascinating psychological character study -- the way the characters interact with each other, and the way their behavior is modified by their surroundings, becomes the core of the story. It felt a little bit like an intro to psychology for teens, but somehow managed to not really feel that dated (esp. considering this book was original published in 1974. I'm curious to see if contemporary teens will like it -- I think they'd be sucked in by the setting, and stay in for the characters.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_660537382">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/660537382" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3178331-michelle" class="left imgcol" title="Michelle"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442499917p2/3178331.jpg" alt="Michelle" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/660537382?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 09, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3178331-michelle" class="user" title="Michelle">Michelle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3178331-michelle?shelf=2013-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3178331-michelle?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer660537382">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4548866748900438275">Lately, I've been re-reading books that I had read as a teen, and I'm happy to say that thirty years later, I still love this book. The amazing thing (to me) is that House of Stairs is just as current today as it was back then. It is set in an unspecified, futuristic setting, yet now its seems very contemporary.<br /><br />House of Stairs, like the Hunger Games series, is a story in which teens are pitted against teens. HoS is much simpler than HG, both in terms of setting and plot, but the four characters</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4548866748900438275">Lately, I've been re-reading books that I had read as a teen, and I'm happy to say that thirty years later, I still love this book. The amazing thing (to me) is that House of Stairs is just as current today as it was back then. It is set in an unspecified, futuristic setting, yet now its seems very contemporary.<br /><br />House of Stairs, like the Hunger Games series, is a story in which teens are pitted against teens. HoS is much simpler than HG, both in terms of setting and plot, but the four characters are very interesting as are the choices they make. This book says a lot about the human condition and reminds me of the themes in other books such as Lord of the Flies and the Butterfly Revolution (another great dystopian story about teens).<br /><br />William Sleator's book was my first experience in dystopia, and I still love the genre. What surprised me, however, was that even though this book was supposedly written for 9 - 12 year olds, the author used *a lot* of cursing, and much of the scientific jargon towards the end would be very confusing. Even thought this is a short, simple read, I'd say it would be better for high school students.<br /><br />All in all, I highly recommend this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4548866748900438275">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_108706373">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/108706373" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1090874-m" class="left imgcol" title="M."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442519879p2/1090874.jpg" alt="M." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/108706373?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 25, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1090874-m" class="user" title="M.">M.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1090874-m?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1090874-m?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer108706373">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3115504607824233155">I don't read much YA fiction, mainly because if I am reading fiction for the narrative, well, to be blunt, I like fucked up shit. Generally fucked up shit does not seep into YA fiction unless it's in a severely stunted fashion (from my experience). However, somebody on some lit blog (Nick Antosca? Is that his name?) mentioned this dude, and then I found out he was gay, and then I found out the plots of some of his stories are <em>awesome</em>, so I grabbed two of his books from the library while I was at</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3115504607824233155">I don't read much YA fiction, mainly because if I am reading fiction for the narrative, well, to be blunt, I like fucked up shit. Generally fucked up shit does not seep into YA fiction unless it's in a severely stunted fashion (from my experience). However, somebody on some lit blog (Nick Antosca? Is that his name?) mentioned this dude, and then I found out he was gay, and then I found out the plots of some of his stories are <em>awesome</em>, so I grabbed two of his books from the library while I was at work. <br /><br />The setting of this book is incredible. An unending series of white stairs in an all white corridor. Pretty ideal, very Ballardesque, very much appealing to my aesthetics. Unfortunately the stairs don't end up playing any real role other than being an environment that is mostly overlooked (which is annoying to me, but probably not most people), but the extremely fucked-up almost nihilistic nature of the rest of the plot sort of makes up for it.<br /><br />Suffice it to say, I was pretty into it. It still felt like YA prose to me (of course, most of the fiction I read is either modern/contemporary French fiction or "experimental" fiction, so maybe I don't know what straight-up "literary fiction" even is any more), but I didn't mind because what was happening had me interested. <br /><br />I have another Sleator book checked out right now, <em>Singularity</em> I think, that I'll probably knock out later this weekend. Fun stuff though!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_534104055">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/534104055" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13536384-ethan" class="left imgcol" title="Ethan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349885607p2/13536384.jpg" alt="Ethan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/534104055?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 12, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13536384-ethan" class="user" title="Ethan">Ethan</a>

          added it




        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer534104055">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17929107284789313012"> House of Stairs was a good science fiction book by William Sleator. Five 16-year-old orphans are separately blindfolded, driven somewhere, put on an elevator not knowing which direction it was going, taken to the house of stairs, and left there. The first two people are a girl named Lola, and a boy named Peter. They don't know why they are at the house of stairs or how they will get out, but they want to find out. They go searching for an escape and find a girl, named Blossom who is eating food</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17929107284789313012"> House of Stairs was a good science fiction book by William Sleator. Five 16-year-old orphans are separately blindfolded, driven somewhere, put on an elevator not knowing which direction it was going, taken to the house of stairs, and left there. The first two people are a girl named Lola, and a boy named Peter. They don't know why they are at the house of stairs or how they will get out, but they want to find out. They go searching for an escape and find a girl, named Blossom who is eating food that is coming from a machine. Lola quickly grabs a piece, and also later gives one to Peter, but the way Lola acted about it made Blossom angry, so Blossom makes it her mission to show everyone how mean Lola is. After a while the machine stops working and they all try to make it work again but it doesn't. And that's when Abigail shows up. As they keep wondering about the machine, and if they will ever get out, another boy named Oliver comes along. Oliver’s cheerfulness brought their spirits up a lot, even though he couldn't make the machine work. But after awhile, they start getting more and more irritated with the little things because they are all so hungry. One time, In the midst of a fight they do something to get the machine to work, and the keep doing exactly what they did to get more and more food but then it suddenly stops coming. Lola thinks it happened because of the fighting but did it really? If it didn't will they ever find what did and eat again? If it did are they all willing to fight each other for food? Will the machine turn them against each other? Will they ever escape? I liked this book a lot, because of its suspension. A lot of the time I couldn't put it down. Also the narration made it interesting because it was always a third person narrator but the perspectives changed often, so the reader can hear different people's thoughts. The reason this book fell under the science fiction genre is the setting. The main characters are in an unknown location place and all they see are stairs. No walls, no ceilings, no floors, just stairs. I give this book a four out of five stars and recommend it to anyone who likes mysterious, suspenseful, science-fiction books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17929107284789313012">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=middle-grade" class="actionLinkLite">middle-grade</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer56271501">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2025704321992583953">I was probably about 10 the first time I picked this up. Then I read it a squillion times. Now I just read it again, as an adult, and I'm pleasantly surprised how well it holds up. 5 teens thrown into a room with nothing but stairs, as far as they can climb in any direction, plus one landing with a little machine on it. The machine will dispense food occasionally, if the teens do exactly what they're supposed to--a series of coordinated movements, from a complicated dance to assaulting each othe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2025704321992583953">I was probably about 10 the first time I picked this up. Then I read it a squillion times. Now I just read it again, as an adult, and I'm pleasantly surprised how well it holds up. 5 teens thrown into a room with nothing but stairs, as far as they can climb in any direction, plus one landing with a little machine on it. The machine will dispense food occasionally, if the teens do exactly what they're supposed to--a series of coordinated movements, from a complicated dance to assaulting each other. But maybe there's a way to beat the machine. Maybe there's a point to all of it. Or maybe there's not.<br /><br />This isn't quite as gripping or fast-paced as I remember it being, possibly because I've read an awful lot more psychological thrillers since being 10 years old, but the ideas are still pretty sharp. And it was still compelling. And, let's face it--it's stuck in my head well enough over these past 20 years that I can recite the final creepy line from memory, and that's not something you can do with a forgettable book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2025704321992583953">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144453152" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4104567-tony-bertauski" class="left imgcol" title="Tony Bertauski"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442468015p2/4104567.jpg" alt="Tony Bertauski" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/144453152?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 30, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4104567-tony-bertauski" class="user" title="Tony Bertauski">Tony Bertauski</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer144453152">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18356934292203412512">MC Escher meets Lord of the Flies. <br /><br />This is the kind of book I love. Drops you right into an unknown, in this case surreal, environment and unfolds from there. Sleator does a great job unveiling his characters through dialog. Each personality is revealed in what they say, how they say it and what they're doing. He wastes few words on back story, instead focuses on the nightmarish reality of the present which contains endless stairs and a food-spitting machine that keeps them dancing for more. Whi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18356934292203412512">MC Escher meets Lord of the Flies. <br /><br />This is the kind of book I love. Drops you right into an unknown, in this case surreal, environment and unfolds from there. Sleator does a great job unveiling his characters through dialog. Each personality is revealed in what they say, how they say it and what they're doing. He wastes few words on back story, instead focuses on the nightmarish reality of the present which contains endless stairs and a food-spitting machine that keeps them dancing for more. While I didn't feel deeply connected with any of the characters, or particularly sympathetic, their motivations were clearly and, to some extent, their personalities.<br /><br />However, the stark world of continuous stairs was not much of a hook. I found it easy to put down in the beginning, but that changed. The end was not the exciting pop I hoped for. In fact, I was a little disappointed. Until the very last sentence. When I put the book down, I was satisfied.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18356934292203412512">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1112773628">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6925706422468863922">Bizarre! I was fascinated by where the plot was going, but ended up a bit underwhelmed by the epilogue. I also didn't feel a connection with any of the characters, but maybe that's asking a lot out of a tiny 166 page book? Anyway, guessing at the "why" of the story, as well as watching their behavior and relationships develop, is all very interesting and quietly horrifying. This probably would've blown my mind as a young teen.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }

    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");

      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/14004.William_Sleator">About William Sleator</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/14004.William_Sleator"><img alt="14004" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1206554364p2/14004.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer10023684143670610017">William Warner Sleator III was born in Havre de Grace, Maryland on February 13, 1945, and moved to St. Louis, MO when he was three. He graduated from University City High School in 1963, from Harvard in 1967 with BAs in music and English. <br /><br />For more than thirty years, William Sleator thrilled readers with his inventive books. His <em>House of Stairs</em> was named one of the best novels of the twentieth cent</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10023684143670610017">William Warner Sleator III was born in Havre de Grace, Maryland on February 13, 1945, and moved to St. Louis, MO when he was three. He graduated from University City High School in 1963, from Harvard in 1967 with BAs in music and English. <br /><br />For more than thirty years, William Sleator thrilled readers with his inventive books. His <em>House of Stairs</em> was named one of the best novels of the twentieth century by the Young Adult Library Services Association. <br /><br />William Sleator died in early August 2011 at his home in Thailand.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10023684143670610017">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/881499-house-of-stairs">Quotes from House of Stairs</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“But really, that is kind of silly,' Abigail tried to explain. 'I mean, a book is much less personal than a programmed screen that can respond to you according to your needs, and concentrate on what's hard for you, and go fast on what's easy. A book stays the same no matter *who's* reading it. And anyway, I don't see how anyone could read a whole long book, it must be so boring!'<br /><br />'But...but it wasn't,' Peter said faintly. 'I...almost forgot I was reading it. The...the whole story was going on in my head.'<br /><br />'I still don't understand,' said Oliver. 'I mean, watching a real-life hologram right before your eyes is better than anything you could *imagine.*”</span>
